The state is ranked fourth in the nation for this kind of mining production.<sup id="cite_ref-mediofisico_13-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mediofisico-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-schmal_15-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmal-15">[15]</a></sup> However, mining only accounts for 1.5% of economic activity for the entire state.<sup id="cite_ref-schmal_15-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmal-15">[15]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Veracruz was a pioneer in both the extraction and refining of petroleum products.<sup id="cite_ref-mediofisico_13-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mediofisico-13">[13]</a></sup> The state has about one-fourth of Mexico\xe2\x80\x99s petroleum reserves and ranks third in petroleum production.<sup id="cite_ref-mediofisico_13-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mediofisico-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-brittanica_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brittanica-14">[14]</a></sup> Most of this production is concentrated in the northern part of the state.<sup id="cite_ref-schmal_15-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmal-15">[15]</a></sup> Approximately 40,000,000 barrels (6,400,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of oil are produced each year and 109,870,000,000 cubic feet (3.111<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of natural gas. Petrochemicals represent 28.1% of the state\xe2\x80\x99s manufacturing and ranks first nationally. There are 22 petrochemical plants, with the most important being <a href="/w/index.php?title=La_Cangrejera&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="La Cangrejera (page does not exist)">La Cangrejera</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1ltipan_de_Morelos" class="mw-redirect" title="J\xc3\xa1ltipan de Morelos">J\xc3\xa1ltipan de Morelos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poza_Rica" title="Poza Rica">Poza Rica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosoleacaque" title="Cosoleacaque">Cosoleacaque</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pajaritos,_Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Pajaritos, Veracruz (page does not exist)">Pajaritos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minatitl%C3%A1n,_Veracruz" title="Minatitl\xc3\xa1n, Veracruz">Minatitl\xc3\xa1n</a>.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_Lane_Oil_Fields">Golden Lane Oil Fields</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Golden Lane Oil Fields">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Golden_Lane_Map.pdf" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Golden_Lane_Map.pdf/page1-300px-Golden_Lane_Map.pdf.jpg" width="300" height="389" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Golden_Lane_Map.pdf/page1-450px-Golden_Lane_Map.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Golden_Lane_Map.pdf/page1-600px-Golden_Lane_Map.pdf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="961" data-file-height="1247" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Golden_Lane_Map.pdf" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nMap of the Golden Lane<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The "Golden Lane" ("Dos Bocas-Alamo structure" or The Ridge") refers to a series of oil fields aligned in an arc onshore and a symmetrical alignment offshore, forming two sides of a <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atoll</a> 180&#160;km long.<sup id="cite_ref-Viniegra_O._p._309_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Viniegra_O._p._309-26">[26]</a></sup> The structure was discovered in 1908 by <a href="/wiki/Weetman_Pearson" class="mw-redirect" title="Weetman Pearson">Weetman Pearson</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Eagle_Petroleum_Company" title="Mexican Eagle Petroleum Company">Mexican Eagle Petroleum Company</a> San diego de la Mar No. 1 well, which flowed 2,500 <a href="/wiki/Bbl" class="mw-redirect" title="Bbl">bbl</a>/day.<sup id="cite_ref-Viniegra_O._p._309_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Viniegra_O._p._309-26">[26]</a></sup> The Golden Lane oil fields penetrate a massive elliptical <a href="/wiki/Reef" title="Reef">reef</a> platform, identified as the Sierra del Abra limestone (consisting of reef, backreef and <a href="/wiki/Lagoon" title="Lagoon">lagoonal</a> <a href="/wiki/Facies" title="Facies">facies</a>), which dips southeast.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The site for the discovery well was selected due to the presence of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_seep" title="Petroleum seep">petroleum seeps</a>, just as was <a href="/wiki/Edward_Laurence_Doheny" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward Laurence Doheny">Edward Laurence Doheny</a>\'s La Paz No. well in the Ebano-Panuco petroleum district west of <a href="/wiki/Tampico,_Tamaulipas" class="mw-redirect" title="Tampico, Tamaulipas">Tampico, Tamaulipas</a>, the first Mexican discovery well in 1904.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In 1908, the infamous <a href="/wiki/Dos_Bocas_oil_fire" title="Dos Bocas oil fire">Dos Bocas oil fire</a> occurred after the San Diego de la Mar No. 3 <a href="/wiki/Blowout_(well_drilling)" title="Blowout (well drilling)">blowout</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Viniegra_O._p._311_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Viniegra_O._p._311-29">[29]</a></sup> This was followed by the Potrero del Llano No. 4 well, flowing 100,000 bbl per day during the three months it was out of control.<sup id="cite_ref-Viniegra_O._p._311_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Viniegra_O._p._311-29">[29]</a></sup> Doheny\'s Cerro Azul No. 4 well, located by Ezequiel Ordonez, became the largest daily production record holder in 1916 at 260,000 bbl.<sup id="cite_ref-Viniegra_O._p._311_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Viniegra_O._p._311-29">[29]</a></sup> Geophysical studies, in particular <a href="/wiki/Gravimetry" title="Gravimetry">gravimetry</a>, starting in 1920 led to the discovery of the Poza Rica Field in 1932 and Moralillo Field in 1948, on the west flank of the Golden Lane in the Tamabra forereef facies.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Refraction <a href="/wiki/Seismography" class="mw-redirect" title="Seismography">seismography</a> surveys started in 1930, augemented in 1948 with reflection seismography, which led to the discovery of the southern extent of the atoll with the Ezequiel Ordonez No. 1 well in 1952 and additional discoveries through 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-Viniegra_O._p._312_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Viniegra_O._p._312-31">[31]</a></sup> Marine seismic and <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometer</a> surveys starting in 1957 showed the extent of the atoll offshore and led to the first offshore well, Isla de Lobos No. 1-B, in 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-Viniegra_O._p._312_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Viniegra_O._p._312-31">[31]</a></sup> The depths of wells are on the order of 500 m onshore and 2000 m offshore.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span id="Industry,_transportation_and_commerce"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry.2C_transportation_and_commerce">Industry, transportation and commerce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Industry, transportation and commerce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Laguna_Verde_Nuclear_Power_Plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Laguna Verde Nuclear Power Plant">Laguna Verde Nuclear Power Plant</a> (LVNPP) in Alto Lucero, Veracruz, produces about 4.5% of Mexico\'s electrical energy.</p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PortVeracruz55.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/PortVeracruz55.JPG/220px-PortVeracruz55.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/PortVeracruz55.JPG/330px-PortVeracruz55.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/PortVeracruz55.JPG/440px-PortVeracruz55.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PortVeracruz55.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nA portion of the port of Veracruz</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The manufacturing industry in Veracruz accounts for between 21% and one-third of the state\'s gross domestic product, and approximately 64% of the manufacturing industry GDP is generated by the chemical and petrochemical sectors.<sup id="cite_ref-schmal_15-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmal-15">[15]</a></sup> Other products produced include metals, processed foods, beverages, printing and publishing, textiles and machinery.<sup id="cite_ref-schmal_15-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmal-15">[15]</a></sup> Most of the state\'s industry takes place in the municipalities of <a href="/wiki/Coatzacoalcos" title="Coatzacoalcos">Coatzacoalcos</a>, Minatitl\xc3\xa1n, Cosoleacaque, Poza Rica, C\xc3\xb3rdoba, Orizaba, Tuxpan and Veracruz, with over 5,000 establishments. The rest is divided among nearly 11,000 smaller establishments. There are five major industrial parks: Bruno Pagliai, Ixtac, Petroquimico Morelos, C\xc3\xb3rdoba-Amatl\xc3\xa1n and Parque 2000. The largest of these is Bruno Pagliai, which covers 300.8 hectares.</p>\n<p>Transportation and commerce are important factors in the state, mostly linked to importing and exporting through its four deepwater ports. The focus of most of these activities is the port of Veracruz. It has the most favored position on Mexico\xe2\x80\x99s Gulf coast and is extensively used for exports to the United States, Latin America and Europe. Seventy-five percent of all port activity in Mexico takes place in Veracruz. The chief exports of this state are coffee, fresh fruits, fertilizer, sugar, fish and crustaceans.<sup id="cite_ref-schmal_15-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmal-15">[15]</a></sup> Other ports include those in Tuxpan and Coatzacoalcos. Most highway, rail and air connections link to the port of Veracruz and other ports to the south.<sup id="cite_ref-brittanica_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brittanica-14">[14]</a></sup> The state has 73 companies that have been classified as high-volume exporters and it is ranked sixth in the country for exports. The state contains five major food wholesale markets, 146 government sponsored markets, about 75,000 private stores and 201 supermarkets. Wholesale vendors focus on agricultural products such as wood, livestock and food products. The major focal point for international business is the World Trade Center EXPOVER in Boca del R\xc3\xado. Inaugurated in 1989, the center has facilities to accommodate 5,000 people in 7,000 m2, an exhibition hall of 12,000m2, a business center and parking for over 800 vehicles.</p>\n<p>In the industrial sector, relatively poor municipios are not catching up to relatively rich ones though the latter are not diverging either. A policy of encouraging much more domestic and/or foreign investment is called for if poorer areas are to prosper and the outflow of residents is to stop.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Handcrafts">Handcrafts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Handcrafts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the more rural and indigenous areas of the state, a number of handicrafts are still made and sold both to local buyers and to tourists. Many of these crafts are produced by communities that specialize in one or more types. Wood furniture and other items are made by the Huasteca people, mostly using cedar and palm trees. The best work comes from the towns of Ozulama and Castillo de Teayo. Teocelo and Monte Blanco are known for bamboo furniture and other items. Musical instruments of wood such as a guitar called \xe2\x80\x9cjarana\xe2\x80\x9d are constructed in the Los Tuxtlas area, especially in Catemaco, with flutes made in Papantla. Wooden masks are made in Teocelo, and items made with the wood of coffee plants are made in Misantla, Coatepec, Huastusco and Xico. Corn husks are used to make decorative figures, often religious, in Nautla and Naranjos de Amatl\xc3\xa1n. Palm fronds are woven into fans, shoes and baskets in Jalcomulco, Ozulama and Tlalixcoyan.<sup id="cite_ref-rincones4042_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones4042-34">[34]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Ceramics have been made in almost all parts of Veracruz since the Olmecs. One area known for its work is Papantla which also includes life sized representations of folk dancers from the area along with more mundane items of glazed and unglazed pottery. Minatitl\xc3\xa1n is known for its production of ceramic cooking utensils which are also popular in the neighboring municipalities of Actopan and Naolinco. San Miguel Aguasuelos and Jalcomulco are known for their white clay wares which include water jars, toys, nativity scenes, bells and more.<sup id="cite_ref-rincones4042_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones4042-34">[34]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Traditional clothing and embroidery can be most easily found in the La Huasteca area, where elaborately decorated women\'s blouses can be seen, especially in the El Higo and Tlalixcoyan area. In Totonacalpan, men are still often seen in white shirts and pants with a bag to hold personal items. This dress dates back to the early colonial period and had not changed much since then. Other areas specialize in wool items such as Naranjos de Amatl\xc3\xa1n, Minatil\xc3\xa1n and the city of Veracruz where items such as dresses, skirts and jackets. These and other textils such as tablecloths and napkins are often decorated with cross-stitch. Leather items include shoes, jackets, bags, wallets, belts and boots and are usually made in the La Huasteca region, Teocolo, Citlalt\xc3\xa9petl and Naolinco.<sup id="cite_ref-rincones4042_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones4042-34">[34]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gastronomy">Gastronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Gastronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG/220px-HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG/330px-HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG/440px-HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nHuachinango (<a href="/wiki/Red_snapper_(fish)" class="mw-redirect" title="Red snapper (fish)">red snapper</a>) a la Veracruzana</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The gastronomy of the state is unique in Mexico and mixed Spanish, indigenous, and other influences.<sup id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup> From the pre-Hispanic period, the cuisine of the state was unique. The staple triumvirate of corn, beans, and squash was supplemented by tropical fruits, vanilla beans, and an herb called acuyo or <a href="/wiki/Hoja_santa" class="mw-redirect" title="Hoja santa">hoja santa</a>. Another important native contribution is <a href="/wiki/Seafood" title="Seafood">seafood</a>, which is featured in many dishes such as, <a href="/wiki/Arroz_a_la_tumbada" title="Arroz a la tumbada">arroz a la tumbada</a> and caldo de mariscos (seafood soup).<sup id="cite_ref-khursh_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-khursh-11">[11]</a></sup></p>\n<p>After the conquest and during the colonial period, many other <a href="/wiki/Spices" class="mw-redirect" title="Spices">spices</a> and ingredients were brought and have had a greater influence in the cooking here than in other parts of the country. From Europe, the Spanish brought <a href="/wiki/Saffron" title="Saffron">saffron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parsley" title="Parsley">parsley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thyme" title="Thyme">thyme</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marjoram" title="Marjoram">marjoram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bay_laurel" class="mw-redirect" title="Bay laurel">bay laurel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cilantro" class="mw-redirect" title="Cilantro">cilantro</a> as well Asian spices such as <a href="/wiki/Cloves" class="mw-redirect" title="Cloves">cloves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cinnamon" title="Cinnamon">cinnamon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black_pepper" title="Black pepper">black pepper</a>. The Spaniards also brought wheat, rice, almonds, olives and olive oil, garlic, and <a href="/wiki/Capers" class="mw-redirect" title="Capers">capers</a>. The latter three are essential ingredients in what is perhaps the most famous specialty of the region, <a href="/wiki/Huachinango_a_la_veracruzana" class="mw-redirect" title="Huachinango a la veracruzana">huachinango a la veracruzana</a>, red snapper in a spicy tomato sauce. Caribbean imports such as <a href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pineapple" title="Pineapple">pineapple</a> were adapted as well as the <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanut</a>, brought from Africa by the Portuguese via Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-khursh_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-khursh-11">[11]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Veracruz cuisine divides into six regions called Sotavento, Centro Norte, Centro Sur, Sierra, Costa Norte, and Los Tuxtlas. The Sotovento area is in the south of the state, and the dishes here are heavily based on <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>. Common dishes include arroz a la tumbada, which is rice cooked with seafood or meat and rice with fried bananas. Seafood dishes are also prominent based mostly on fish and shrimp. A common ingredient in dishes is a herb called \xe2\x80\x9choja santa\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9chierba Santa,\xe2\x80\x9d which is a plant of the <a href="/wiki/Piperaceae" title="Piperaceae">Piperaceae</a> family. The Centro Norte is centered on Xalapa. Dishes here tend to be more indigenous in nature, heavily flavored with mild chili peppers. Common dishes here include Chilehuates, similar to a tamale, stuffed chile peppers, and enchiladas. Less seafood and more pork and domestic fowl are consumed. The Centro Sur area is mostly indigenous and encompasses the area of, Huatusco, Coscomatepec, Cotaxtla, Orizaba, Amatl\xc3\xa1n, Huilango y las espa\xc3\xb1olas C\xc3\xb3rdoba y Fort\xc3\xadn de las Flores, la negra Yanga and San Lorenzo de los Negros. Dishes here are similar to Centro Norte, but <a href="/wiki/Chayote" title="Chayote">chayotes</a> appear more often as this region is a major producer of the vegetable. Meats in <a href="/wiki/Adobo" title="Adobo">adobo</a> sauce are common as well. The Sierra and Costa Norte encompass the northern part of the state, such as the P\xc3\xa1nuco River area and Totonacapan. This area is noted for a number of unique dishes such as frijoles en achuchutl, made with black beans, pork rind, <a href="/wiki/Chayote" title="Chayote">chayotes</a>, squash seeds, and <a href="/wiki/Jalape%C3%B1o_pepper" class="mw-redirect" title="Jalape\xc3\xb1o pepper">jalape\xc3\xb1o peppers</a>. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bocole&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bocole (page does not exist)">Bocoles</a> are a kind of filled tortilla made with corn dough, stuffed with black beans, chorizo, eggs, or seafood, which then are fried in lard. Tamales are often made with banana leaves. The area is also known for its breads, especially <a href="/wiki/Anise" title="Anise">anise</a>\xe2\x80\x93flavored rolls. The Los Tuxtlas area is centered on the communities of Santiago, San Andr\xc3\xa9s and Catemaco, which were the center of the Olmec civilization. The cuisine in this area features yucca, \xe2\x80\x9cchocos\xe2\x80\x9d (a type edible flower), fish, especially mojarra, and exotic meats such as monkey, and <a href="/wiki/Iguana" title="Iguana">iguana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Museums">Museums</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Museums">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg/300px-San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg" width="300" height="430" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg/450px-San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg/600px-San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1080" data-file-height="1548" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe Olmec <a href="/wiki/San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1" class="mw-redirect" title="San Martin Pajapan Monument 1">San Martin Pajapan Monument 1</a> on exhibit in the Museum of Anthropology in Xalapa</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The state capital of Xalapa is also home to a number of important museums. The Museum of Anthropology contains the second most important collection of Mesoamerican artifacts in the country. It was built beginning in 1959 over six hectares. The complex is divided into to various halls and galleries by theme, focusing on the Olmec and Totonac cultures. The Patio Olmeca contains the colossal head found in 1945 and known as El Rey (The King). Other important artifacts include giant stelae and <a href="/wiki/San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1" class="mw-redirect" title="San Martin Pajapan Monument 1">San Martin Pajapan Monument 1</a> (at right). The Museum of Science and Technology is in Xalapa. It contains more than 400 exhibitions in eight halls: Life, Ecology, Space, Transportation, Sciences, Energy, Water and Earth. The Pinoteca Diega Rivera was established by the state in the former Monastery of San Francisco in Xalapa. Its primary function is to preserve and display <a href="/wiki/Diego_Rivera" title="Diego Rivera">Rivera</a>\xe2\x80\x99s works owned by the state, but it also contains early works by <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Velasco_G%C3%B3mez" title="Jos\xc3\xa9 Mar\xc3\xada Velasco G\xc3\xb3mez">Jos\xc3\xa9 Mar\xc3\xada Velasco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Cuesta" title="Jorge Cuesta">Jorge Cuesta</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Teodoro_A._Dehesa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Teodoro A. Dehesa (page does not exist)">Teodoro A. Dehesa</a>. Near the city is the Hacienda del Lencero, which was the home and headquarters of President Antonio L\xc3\xb3pez de Santa Anna in the 19th century. It has been preserved and turned into a museum.<sup id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In Tuxpan is the Regional Museum of Anthropology with more than 400 pieces from pre-Hispanic groups in the region, displayed in four halls. Most of the pieces come from the center of the state and from the Huasteca region.On the edge of the Tuxpan River.The city also has the Mexican-Cuban Museum. It contains a collection of photographs from the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Revolution" title="Cuban Revolution">Cuban Revolution</a> as well as a statue of <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mart%C3%AD" title="Jos\xc3\xa9 Mart\xc3\xad">Jos\xc3\xa9 Mart\xc3\xad</a> .<sup id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Other museums are scattered in other parts of the state. The <a href="/wiki/Salvador_Ferrando" title="Salvador Ferrando">Salvador Ferrando</a> Museum is located in <a href="/wiki/Tlacotalpan,_Veracruz" class="mw-redirect" title="Tlacotalpan, Veracruz">Tlacotalpan</a> and contains many everyday items and art from the 16th to the 19th centuries.The Tuxteco Regional Museum in Santiago de Tuxtla contains Olmec and Totonac artifacts including art objects, farming implements, utensils and more. In the garden area, there are giant stone sculptures from the Tres Zapotes site. The Jard\xc3\xadn Central (Central Garden) of the municipality of <a href="/wiki/Tierra_Blanca,_Veracruz" title="Tierra Blanca, Veracruz">Tierra Blanca</a> has a number of Totonac archeological pieces on display. The Archeological Museum of C\xc3\xb3rdoba is in the city of the same name with three exhibition halls containing artifacts from the Maya, Olmec, Toltec and Huastec cultures. There is also a collection of historical photographs of the city. The Veracruz State Art Museum (Museo de Arte del Estado de Veracruz) is located in Orizaba in what was the monastery associated with the Concordia Church. Its collection includes works by Diego Rivera, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ignacio_Rosas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ignacio Rosas (page does not exist)">Ignacio Rosas</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gonzalo_Arg%C3%BCelles&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Gonzalo Arg\xc3\xbcelles (page does not exist)">Gonzalo Arg\xc3\xbcelles</a>. It also contains a collection of historical photographs related to Veracruz and art. The Malintzin Archeological Museum is in the municipality of <a href="/wiki/Nogales,_Veracruz" title="Nogales, Veracruz">Nogales</a>. It is a small museum with one hall, with photographs and documents. It is located in the church where <a href="/wiki/Malinche" class="mw-redirect" title="Malinche">Malinche</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Juan_de_Jaramillo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Juan de Jaramillo (page does not exist)">Juan de Jaramillo</a> were supposedly married.<sup id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fairs_and_festivals">Fairs and festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Fairs and festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The state is noted for its quantity and variety of festivals. The most important of these is <a href="/wiki/Carnival" title="Carnival">Carnival</a> in the city of Veracruz. This city\xe2\x80\x99s version of the event begins with the \xe2\x80\x9cburning of bad humor,\xe2\x80\x9d which is represented in effigy. A number of kings and queens are "crowned" including categories for children but the most important is the Rey Feo (Ugly King) and the Reina del Carnaval (Queen of the Carnival). The latter is accompanies by cadets from the Naval Academy during the parade. This celebration is repeated all along the Veracruz coastline with other significant festivities taking place in <a href="/wiki/Alvarado,_Veracruz" title="Alvarado, Veracruz">Alvarado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coatzacoalcos" title="Coatzacoalcos">Coatzacoalcos</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Rafael,_Veracruz" title="San Rafael, Veracruz">San Rafael</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Cardel,_Veracruz" title="Jos\xc3\xa9 Cardel, Veracruz">Villa Jos\xc3\xa9 Cardel</a>. <a href="/wiki/Minatitl%C3%A1n,_Veracruz" title="Minatitl\xc3\xa1n, Veracruz">Minatitl\xc3\xa1n</a>\'s celebration draws people from the nearby states of Oaxaca and Tabasco.<sup id="cite_ref-rincones23_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones23-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Day_of_the_Dead" title="Day of the Dead">Day of the Dead</a> is celebrated in almost all of Mexico from 31 Oct to 2 November but there are local twists in the state. In some places, it is commemorated during the months of August and September. In Papantla, boards or tables are placed on rooftops, which have been adorned with flowers, plant matter and more. In Tantoyuca, it is commemorated with costumes and music, similar to Carnival.<sup id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The Christian celebration of <a href="/wiki/Candlemas" title="Candlemas">Candlemas</a> is fused with traditions associated with <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chalchiuhtlicua&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Chalchiuhtlicua (page does not exist)">Chalchiuhtlicua</a>, the goddess of water, rivers, lakes and ocean. She was replaced by the Virgin of Candlemas, the protector of fishermen, making this celebration particularly important on the coast, especially in Tlacotalpan, where it is celebrated with much pomp. In <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1ltipan_de_Morelos" class="mw-redirect" title="J\xc3\xa1ltipan de Morelos">J\xc3\xa1ltipan de Morelos</a>, ethnic <a href="/wiki/Nahua_peoples" title="Nahua peoples">Nahuas</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Popolucas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Popolucas (page does not exist)">Popolucas</a> dress in elaborate costumes and arrange their hair in intricate styles. In Santa Mar\xc3\xada Magdalena, on 22 July, bulls are set free to roam the streets. Corn harvest festivals are prominent in the Huasteca region in municipalities such as <a href="/wiki/Chontla" title="Chontla">Chontla</a>, Chicontepec, and <a href="/wiki/Ixhuatl%C3%A1n_de_Madero" title="Ixhuatl\xc3\xa1n de Madero">Ixhuatl\xc3\xa1n de Madero</a>. These generally include native dances and foods based on corn.<sup id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rincones23_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones23-36">[36]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dance_and_music">Dance and music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Dance and music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small-left" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;float:left">\n<tr>\n<td class="mbox-image">\n<div class="center">\n<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div>\n</div>\n</td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">\n<div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Malague_a_Master_44.ogg" title="File:Malague a Master 44.ogg">La malague\xc3\xb1a</a></div>\n<div>\n<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="210.85931972789" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Malague_a_Master_44.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons">\n<source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c9/Malague_a_Master_44.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (104 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="103523" /></audio></div>\n</div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Son huasteco, performed by Los Hijos de la Malinche of Jalitpan, Veracruz</div>\n</div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em">\n<hr />\n<span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<p>The state is well known in the country for its music and dance. The <a href="/wiki/Fandango" title="Fandango">fandango</a> is a dance brought over from Spanish. Today the state has two varieties: the jarocho and the huasteco.<sup id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup> Indigenous and folk dances in the state are most often associated with rituals and religious festivals. These include one called Los Lisceres also called the Tigres from the Los Tuxtlas region. Participants wear Olmec style masks which represent the rain god Tlal\xc3\xb3c. Another is Los Guaguas in which the participants pay homage to the sun, and Los Santiagos, which is related to the veneration of Saint James, patron saint of Hern\xc3\xa1n Cort\xc3\xa9s. One last one is called Los Negritos (The Little Black Ones) which was created by African slaves. According to tradition, the origin of this dance lies in a story about a boy who was bitten by a snake and the rituals his family held to heal him.<sup id="cite_ref-rincones22_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones22-37">[37]</a></sup></p>\n<p>However, the state\xe2\x80\x99s most famous dance is as much a ritual and daredevil act as movements performed to music. The <b>Danza de los Voladores</b> (Dance of the Flyers) is a ceremony/ritual which has its roots in the pre-Hispanic period and presently best known as associated with the town of <a href="/wiki/Papantla,_Veracruz" class="mw-redirect" title="Papantla, Veracruz">Papantla</a>, Veracruz. It is believed to have originated with the <a href="/wiki/Nahua_peoples" title="Nahua peoples">Nahua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huastec_people" title="Huastec people">Huastec</a> and <a href="/wiki/Otomi_people" title="Otomi people">Otomi</a> peoples in central Mexico, and then spread throughout most of <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>. The ritual consists of dance and the climbing of a 30-meter pole from which four of the five participants then launch themselves tied with ropes to descend to the ground. The fifth remains on top of the pole, dancing and playing a flute and drum. According to myth, the ritual was created to ask the gods to end a severe <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>. Although the ritual did not originate with the <a href="/wiki/Totonac" class="mw-redirect" title="Totonac">Totonac</a> people, today it is most strongly associated with them, especially those in and around <a href="/wiki/Papantla,_Veracruz" class="mw-redirect" title="Papantla, Veracruz">Papantla</a>, as the ceremony has died off in most other places.<sup id="cite_ref-wilkerson7576_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilkerson7576-38">[38]</a></sup> The ceremony was named an <a href="/wiki/Intangible_Cultural_Heritage" class="mw-redirect" title="Intangible Cultural Heritage">Intangible Cultural Heritage</a> by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> in order to help the ritual survive and thrive in the modern world.<sup id="cite_ref-mcclat_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcclat-39">[39]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The state\'s best-known musical style is called the "son". A \xe2\x80\x9c<a href="/wiki/Son_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Son (music)">son</a>\xe2\x80\x9d is a musical variation which traces its origins to Spain and developed during the 17th and 18th centuries. It is the state\xe2\x80\x99s most popular musical style shows influences from the many peoples who have lived here such as indigenous groups, Portuguese, Italians, Africans, French and others. The music is generally performed by harps, violins and guitars, with an occasional wind instrument. <a href="/wiki/Huapango" title="Huapango">Son huasteco (also called son huapango)</a> is a variety of son played in the north of the state mostly among the Totonacs. Son jarocho is the better known and more popular variety played in the south of the state. The famous Grammy award-winning song "La Bamba" by <a href="/wiki/Los_Lobos" title="Los Lobos">Los Lobos</a> is said have its roots in a traditional folk song from Veracruz (hence the reference to the "Marinero" in that song).<sup id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rincones2021_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones2021-40">[40]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The state has produced a number of musicians famous in the country. One of the best known is Francisco Gabilondo Soler. Gabilondo Soler is best known for creating a character known as \xe2\x80\x9cCri-cri\xe2\x80\x9d, a singing cricket for a radio show in the first half of the 20th century. As a musician, he specialized in writing children\xe2\x80\x99s songs such as "Abuelito", "Caminito de la escuela", "El baile de los mu\xc3\xb1ecos", "El burrito" and "La negrita Cucurumb\xc3\xa9". A number of his works have been translated into other languages. Another famous musician is Agust\xc3\xadn Lara, who has had more international fame. Nicknamed "Flaco de oro" (golden skinny one), he always insisted that he was born in Tlacotalpan, Veracruz and not Mexico City as records show. Lara formed his first band in 1930 called El Son de Marab\xc3\xba and toured almost continuously in Mexico and abroad during his career. His most famous compositions include "Veracruz", "Noche de Ronda" and "Solamente una vez".<sup id="cite_ref-rincones2021_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones2021-40">[40]</a></sup> Other prominent musicians include To\xc3\xb1a "La Negra" or Mar\xc3\xada Antonia del Carmen Peregino, Narcisco Serradel, Lorenzo Barcelata and Mar\xc3\xada Greever.<sup id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Art and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG/220px-MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG/330px-MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG/440px-MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nMural depicting the history of Papantla in the town square by Teodoro Cano Garc\xc3\xada</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>During the colonial era, a movement called the \xe2\x80\x9cestilo veracruzano\xe2\x80\x9d (Veracruz style) developed mostly focusing on landscapes in the state with a certain amount of indigenous influence although the painters themselves were <i>criollo</i> or Mexico-born Spanish. These paintings focus on the mountains, valles, coasts, volcanos and other natural phenomena in the state. Most of the proponents of this were born in Veracruz itself and include <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jos%C3%A9_Justo_Montiel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jos\xc3\xa9 Justo Montiel (page does not exist)">Jos\xc3\xa9 Justo Montiel</a> (1824\xe2\x80\x931899), <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gonzalo_Arg%C3%BCelles_Bringas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Gonzalo Arg\xc3\xbcelles Bringas (page does not exist)">Gonzalo Arg\xc3\xbcelles Bringas</a> (1877\xe2\x80\x931942), <a href="/w/index.php?title=Eugenio_Landecio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Eugenio Landecio (page does not exist)">Eugenio Landecio</a> (teacher of <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Velasco_G%C3%B3mez" title="Jos\xc3\xa9 Mar\xc3\xada Velasco G\xc3\xb3mez">Jos\xc3\xa9 Mar\xc3\xada Velasco</a>), <a href="/w/index.php?title=Natal_Pesado&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Natal Pesado (page does not exist)">Natal Pesado</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ignacio_Rosas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ignacio Rosas (page does not exist)">Ignacio Rosas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rincones1215_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones1215-41">[41]</a></sup></p>\n<p>However, most of Veracruz\xe2\x80\x99s best-known artists are from the 19th and 20th centuries. In the 19th century, these include <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_Mata_Reyes&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Miguel Mata Reyes (page does not exist)">Miguel Mata Reyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salvador_Ferrando" title="Salvador Ferrando">Salvador Ferrando</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Jara" title="Jos\xc3\xa9 Mar\xc3\xada Jara">Jos\xc3\xa9 Mar\xc3\xada Jara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enrique_Guerra" title="Enrique Guerra">Enrique Guerra</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Alberto_Fuster&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Alberto Fuster (page does not exist)">Alberto Fuster</a>. Miguel Mata Reyes is best known for his contributions to the design of the Palacio de Bellas Artes as well a portrait of <a href="/wiki/Antonio_L%C3%B3pez_de_Santa_Anna" title="Antonio L\xc3\xb3pez de Santa Anna">Antonio L\xc3\xb3pez de Santa Anna</a>. Salvador Ferrando was a portrait and landscape artist from the north of the state. Until recently, most of his work had been hidden in a museum named after him in the Tlacotalpan region. Much of it now is on display at the Museo de Arte de Veracruz in Orizaba. Jos\xc3\xa9 Mar\xc3\xada Jara is noted for his paintings of Veracruz customs, whose works include <i>El Velorio</i>, which was presented at the World\xe2\x80\x99s Fair in Paris. Enrique Guerra was an important sculptor at the end of the 19th century. His best-known works are bas reliefs and include <i>Asesinato de C\xc3\xa9sar</i>, <i>Coroliano</i>, <i>Thais</i> and <i>Cris\xc3\xa1lida</i>. Alberto Fuster was most active at the end of the century and is noted for bringing symbolism painting to Mexico from his stay in Europe. His works include <i>El progreso</i>, <i>Safo en el templo de Delfos</i> and <i>Nativa con loro</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-rincones1215_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones1215-41">[41]</a></sup></p>\n<p>There are three important artists from the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Bracho" title="Carlos Bracho">Carlos Bracho</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Norberto_Mart%C3%ADnez&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Norberto Mart\xc3\xadnez (page does not exist)">Norberto Mart\xc3\xadnez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teodoro_Cano_Garc%C3%ADa" title="Teodoro Cano Garc\xc3\xada">Teodoro Cano Garc\xc3\xada</a>. Active in the first half of the century, sculptor Carlos Bracho\xe2\x80\x99s work has been compared to that of <a href="/wiki/Juan_Rulfo" title="Juan Rulfo">Juan Rulfo</a>. His works have been done in plaster, bronze, terracotta and green onyx and include monumental works which can be found in the cities of Xalapa, <a href="/wiki/Puebla,_Puebla" class="mw-redirect" title="Puebla, Puebla">Puebla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pachuca" title="Pachuca">Pachuca</a> and Mexico City. His best-known works are El abrazo, Cabeza verde and El campesino se apodera de la tierra. Norberto Mart\xc3\xadnez only lived 45 years but is considered one of the most prolific of Mexican painters who dedicated most of his works to social themes. A number of these are early murals such as El comercio in the J\xc3\xa1uregi de Xalapa market as well as an untitled work in a private home in C\xc3\xb3rdoba which deals with the fusion of the Spanish, indigenous and African races in Mexico. Later works include the three murals in the main stairwell of the School of Law at the <a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Xalapa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="University of Xalapa (page does not exist)">University of Xalapa</a> and El hombre y el conocimiento at the <a href="/wiki/Universidad_Veracruzana" title="Universidad Veracruzana">Universidad Veracruzana</a>. Teodoro Cano Garc\xc3\xada is one of Mexico\xe2\x80\x99s most famous muralists of the late 20th century, famous for the promotion of the <a href="/wiki/Totonac_people" title="Totonac people">Totonac</a> culture of his hometown of Papantla. He has created paintings, sculptures, etchings, photography and mixed media works with his murals and sculptures most acclaimed. Examples of his work can be seen in various parts of public buildings in <a href="/wiki/Papantla,_Veracruz" class="mw-redirect" title="Papantla, Veracruz">Papantla</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rincones1215_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones1215-41">[41]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Most of Veracruz\xe2\x80\x99s older architecture can be found in the inland cities of Xalapa and C\xc3\xb3rdoba. Despite being the first Spanish settlement, the city of Veracruz lost most its older structures to the various invasions it has suffered. Architecture from the 16th to the 19th century includes colonial Spanish, Moorish, Neo gothic and Neoclassical. From the 20th century on, a number of names stand out. Armando Bravo Ram\xc3\xadrez remodeled the State Government Palace and the facade of the Capillas de \xc3\x81nimas, both in Xalapa. Other prominent names responsible for many projects in the state include Luis Gonz\xc3\xa1lez Aparicio, Bernal Lascura\xc3\xadn Rangel and Luis Manuel Tello Deschamps.<sup id="cite_ref-rincones1215_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones1215-41">[41]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The literary arts reached their peak in Veracruz starting in the 19th century and extends to the \xe2\x80\x9cGeneration of the 1950s.\xe2\x80\x9d <a href="/wiki/Salvador_D%C3%ADaz_Mir%C3%B3n" title="Salvador D\xc3\xadaz Mir\xc3\xb3n">Salvador D\xc3\xadaz Mir\xc3\xb3n</a> is one of Veracruz\xe2\x80\x99s most-distinguished poets. Over his lifetime from the latter 19th to early 20th centuries, he worked as a professor, politician and journalist contributing to periodicals such as El Veracruzano, El Orden, and El Imparcial. His creative works include some of the first Romantic pieces produced in Mexico such as Oda a V\xc3\xadctor Hugo, Ojos verdes, Gloria and Voces interiores. Other works include <i>Poes\xc3\xadas A Tirsa</i>, <i>Nox</i> (also known as <i>Claudia</i>) and his last works such as <i>Al buen cura</i> and <i>La mujer de nieve</i>. He became a member of the <a href="/wiki/Academia_Mexicana_de_la_Lengua" title="Academia Mexicana de la Lengua">Academia Mexicana de la Lengua</a> and is buried at the <a href="/wiki/Rotonda_de_los_Hombres_Ilustres" class="mw-redirect" title="Rotonda de los Hombres Ilustres">Rotonda de los Hombres Ilustres</a> in Mexico City. <a href="/wiki/Mar%C3%ADa_Enriqueta_Camarillo" title="Mar\xc3\xada Enriqueta Camarillo">Mar\xc3\xada Enriqueta Camarillo</a> was one of a number of women writers to gain prominence in Mexico at the end of the 19th century. While she wrote a number of works such as <i>Jir\xc3\xb3n del mundo</i>, <i>Sorpresas de la vida</i> and <i>El Secreto</i>, she is best known for <i>Rosas de la infancia</i>, with which many Mexicans learned to read.<sup id="cite_ref-rincones1618_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones1618-42">[42]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Writers born at the end of the 19th century, such as <a href="/wiki/Gregorio_L%C3%B3pez_y_Fuentes" class="mw-redirect" title="Gregorio L\xc3\xb3pez y Fuentes">Gregorio L\xc3\xb3pez y Fuentes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Maples_Arce" title="Manuel Maples Arce">Manuel Maples Arce</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jorge_Mateo_Cuesta_Porte-Petit&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jorge Mateo Cuesta Porte-Petit (page does not exist)">Jorge Mateo Cuesta Porte-Petit</a> were often concerned with social issues. Works of this type include El Indio by L\xc3\xb3pez, Metr\xc3\xb3polis by Maples and a poem called <i>Canto a un dios mineral</i> by Cuesta. The following generation, born in the first decades of the 20th century, became known as the Generation of the 1950s. During this time Veracruz\xe2\x80\x99s literary tradition consolidated and decided to break type. One example is <a href="/w/index.php?title=Juan_Hern%C3%A1ndez_Ram%C3%ADrez&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Juan Hern\xc3\xa1ndez Ram\xc3\xadrez (page does not exist)">Juan Hern\xc3\xa1ndez Ram\xc3\xadrez</a>\xe2\x80\x99s writing of poetry in the <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a> spoken in the <a href="/wiki/La_Huasteca" title="La Huasteca">La Huasteca</a> area of Veracruz. One important name from this generation is <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Carballido" title="Emilio Carballido">Emilio Carballido</a> who wrote about 100 plays as well as scripts for radio and television. Some of his works include <i>Rosalba y los llaveros</i>, <i>Felicidad</i> and <i>Las visitaciones del Diablo</i>. In 1996, he won the <a href="/wiki/National_Prize_of_Arts_and_Sciences_(Mexico)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Prize of Arts and Sciences (Mexico)">Premio Nacional de Literatura</a> and in 2002 he received the <a href="/wiki/Ariel_de_Oro" class="mw-redirect" title="Ariel de Oro">Ariel de Oro</a> for his work in cinema.<sup id="cite_ref-rincones1618_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincones1618-42">[42]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The patron saint of Veracruz is <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Gu%C3%ADzar_y_Valencia" title="Rafael Gu\xc3\xadzar y Valencia">Rafael Gu\xc3\xadzar y Valencia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Public education in the state is supervised by the state Direci\xc3\xb3n General de Educaci\xc3\xb3n Popular and the Direcci\xc3\xb3n General de Educaci\xc3\xb3n Media Superior y Superior. The current system is the result of a number of reforms which took place in the 1980s and 1990s. In the late 1990s, 302 new school campuses were created statewide and 257 schools were remodeled. These included new schools for special education, distance learning and technological institutes, giving the state one of the highest number of school campuses in the country. There are a total of 20,479 schools, with nearly 2 million students and about 85,000 teachers. 93% of schools are in the basic education category (preschool, primary and middle schools. Preschools also include those geared towards the indigenous populations, focusing on bilingual and bicultural education in both the indigenous language/culture and Spanish. One major focus of these and other schools is to eliminate illiteracy in indigenous communities. The "Medio Superior" level includes vocational high school and technical colleges. These account for 6.6% of schools in the public system. The Superior level includes teachers\xe2\x80\x99 colleges and universities. There are 166 institutes at this level, with about 68,000 students studying 221 different majors. There are also 63 master\xe2\x80\x99s degree programs and six PhDs.<sup id="cite_ref-infraes_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infraes-44">[44]</a></sup> These institutions serve about 135,000 students accounting for about 19% of the college-aged population (19- to 24-year-olds), slightly below the national average of 24%.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-45">[45]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The major state university is the <a href="/wiki/Universidad_Veracruzana" title="Universidad Veracruzana">Universidad Veracruzana</a>, with offers 56 bachelor\'s degrees, 37 masters and 5 PhDs. It is based in the capital of Xalapa and is noted for its large and varied sports programs. There are campuses in fourteen other cities.<sup id="cite_ref-infraes_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infraes-44">[44]</a></sup> About 37% of university students attend the main public university, with a student population of 47,000 undergraduates and 2,000 postgraduates.<sup id="cite_ref-OECD_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-45">[45]</a></sup> Other important schools include Instituto Tecnologico de Veracruz in Veracruz, Universidad An\xc3\xa1huac in Xalapa, Universidad de Xalapa in Xalapa, <a href="/wiki/ITESM" class="mw-redirect" title="ITESM">ITESM</a> in C\xc3\xb3rdoba, Universidad Crist\xc3\xb3bal Col\xc3\xb3n in Veracruz, the <a href="/wiki/Veracruz_Naval_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Veracruz Naval Academy">Veracruz Naval Academy</a> and the Instituto Tecnol\xc3\xb3gico del Mar.<sup id="cite_ref-infraes_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infraes-44">[44]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="toccolours" style="width:15em;border-spacing: 0;float:right;clear:right;margin:0 0 1em 1em;">\n<tr>\n<th class="navbox-title" colspan="3" style="padding:0.25em;font-size:110%">Historical population</th>\n</tr>\n<tr style="font-size:95%">\n<th style="border-bottom:1px solid
